margin: 0;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td valign="top">\n<p><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #940000; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#FA0000; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Luna_programme" title="Luna programme">Luna programme</a> (USSR)</span><br/>\n<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #E6AC00; padding:1px .6em; background-color:gold; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_3" title="Chang\'e 3">Chang\'e 3</a> (China)</span>\n</p>\n</td>\n<td valign="top">\n<p><span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #00B3B3; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#00FFFF; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Surveyor_program" title="Surveyor program">Surveyor program</a> (US)</span><br/>\n<span style="margin:0; font-size:90%; white-space:nowrap;"><span class="legend-text" style="border:1px solid #2D276D; padding:1px .6em; background-color:#7F78CE; color:black; font-size:95%; line-height:1.25; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a> (US)</span>\n</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>\n<dl><dd>Dates are landing dates in <a href="/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time" title="Coordinated Universal Time">Coordinated Universal Time</a>. Except for the Apollo program, all soft landings were unmanned.</dd></dl>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Luna_2" title="Luna 2">Luna 2</a> was the first man-made object to reach the surface of the Moon (bottom-left).</li>\n<li>Still frame from a video transmission, taken moments before <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a> became the first human to step onto the surface of the Moon, at 02:56 UTC on 21 July 1969. An estimated 500&#160;million people worldwide watched this event, the largest television audience for a live broadcast at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></li></ul>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>A <b>Moon landing</b> is the arrival of a <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a> on the surface of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. This includes both manned and unmanned (robotic) missions. The first human-made object to reach the surface of the Moon was the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Luna_2" title="Luna 2">Luna 2</a> mission, on 13 September 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The United States\' <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a> was the first manned mission to land on the Moon, on 20 July 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> There have been <a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_missions#Manned_Apollo_missions" title="List of Apollo missions">six manned U.S. landings</a> (between 1969 and 1972) and numerous unmanned landings, with no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_landing_(rocketry)" title="Soft landing (rocketry)">soft landings</a> happening from 22 August 1976 until 14 December 2013.\n</p><p>To date, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> is the only country to have successfully conducted manned missions to the Moon, with the last departing the lunar surface in December 1972. All manned and unmanned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_landing_(rocketry)" title="Soft landing (rocketry)">soft landings</a> had taken place on the <a href="/wiki/Near_side_of_the_Moon" title="Near side of the Moon">near side of the Moon</a>, until 3 January 2019 when the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_4" title="Chang\'e 4">Chang\'e 4</a> spacecraft made the first landing on the <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">far side of the Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-far_side_of_the_moon_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-far_side_of_the_moon-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div landings</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Late_20th_century&#8211;Early_21st_century_unmanned_crash_landings"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Late 20th century&#8211;Early 21st century unmanned crash landings</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Hiten_(Japan)"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Hiten (Japan)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Lunar_Prospector_(US)"><span class="tocnumber">14.2</span> <span class="toctext">Lunar Prospector (US)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#SMART-1_(ESA)"><span class="tocnumber">14.3</span> <span class="toctext">SMART-1 (ESA)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#SELENE_(Japan)"><span class="tocnumber">14.4</span> <span class="toctext">SELENE (Japan)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Chang\'e_1_(China)"><span class="tocnumber">14.5</span> <span class="toctext">Chang\'e 1 (China)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Chandrayaan-1_(India)"><span class="tocnumber">14.6</span> <span class="toctext">Chandrayaan-1 (India)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#LCROSS_(US)"><span class="tocnumber">14.7</span> <span class="toctext">LCROSS (US)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#GRAIL_(US)"><span class="tocnumber">14.8</span> <span class="toctext">GRAIL (US)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#LADEE_(US)"><span class="tocnumber">14.9</span> <span class="toctext">LADEE (US)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#21st_century_unmanned_soft_landings"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">21st century unmanned soft landings</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Chang\'e_3_(China)"><span class="tocnumber">15.1</span> <span class="toctext">Chang\'e 3 (China)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#Chang\'e_4_(China)"><span class="tocnumber">15.2</span> <span class="toctext">Chang\'e 4 (China)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Landings_on_moons_of_other_Solar_System_bodies"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Landings on moons of other Solar System bodies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Proposed_future_missions"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">Proposed future missions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Historical_empirical_evidence"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">Historical empirical evidence</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">19</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#References_and_notes"><span class="tocnumber">20</span> <span class="toctext">References and notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">21</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Unmanned_landings">Unmanned landings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Unmanned landings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>After the unsuccessful attempt by the <a href="/wiki/Luna_1" title="Luna 1">Luna 1</a> to land on the Moon in 1959, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> performed the first hard (unpowered) Moon landing later that same year with the <a href="/wiki/Luna_2" title="Luna 2">Luna 2</a> spacecraft, a feat the U.S. duplicated in 1962 with <a href="/wiki/Ranger_4" title="Ranger 4">Ranger 4</a>. Since then, twelve Soviet and U.S. spacecraft have used braking rockets to make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_landing_(rocketry)" title="Soft landing (rocketry)">soft landings</a> and perform scientific operations on the lunar surface, between 1966 and 1976. In 1966 the USSR accomplished the first soft landings and took the first pictures from the lunar surface during the <a href="/wiki/Luna_9" title="Luna 9">Luna 9</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luna_13" title="Luna 13">Luna 13</a> missions. The U.S. followed with five unmanned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surveyor_Program" title="Surveyor Program">Surveyor</a> soft landings.\n</p><p>The Soviet Union achieved the first unmanned lunar soil <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_return_mission" title="Sample return mission">sample return</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Luna_16" title="Luna 16">Luna 16</a> probe on 24 September 1970. This was followed by <a href="/wiki/Luna_20" title="Luna 20">Luna 20</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luna_24" title="Luna 24">Luna 24</a> in 1972 and 1976, respectively. Following the failure at launch in 1969 of the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunokhod" title="Lunokhod">Lunokhod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luna_E-8_No.201" title="Luna E-8 No.201">Luna E-8 No.201</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Luna_17" title="Luna 17">Luna 17</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luna_21" title="Luna 21">Luna 21</a> were successful unmanned <a href="/wiki/Lunar_rover" title="Lunar rover">lunar rover</a> missions in 1970 and 1973.\n</p><p>Many missions were failures at launch.  In addition, several unmanned landing missions achieved the Lunar surface but were unsuccessful, including: <a href="/wiki/Luna_15" title="Luna 15">Luna 15</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luna_18" title="Luna 18">Luna 18</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Luna_23" title="Luna 23">Luna 23</a> all crashed on landing; and the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Surveyor_4" title="Surveyor 4">Surveyor 4</a> lost all radio contact only moments before its landing.\n</p><p>More recently, other nations have crashed spacecraft on the surface of the Moon at speeds of around 8,000 kilometres per hour (5,000&#160;mph), often at precise, planned locations. These have generally been end-of-life lunar orbiters that, because of system degradations, could no longer overcome <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit#Perturbation_effects" title="Lunar orbit">perturbations</a> from lunar <a href="/wiki/Mass_concentration_(astronomy)" title="Mass concentration (astronomy)">mass concentrations</a> ("masscons") to maintain their orbit. Japan\'s lunar orbiter <a href="/wiki/Hiten" title="Hiten">Hiten</a> impacted the Moon\'s surface on 10 April 1993. The <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> performed a controlled crash impact with their orbiter <a href="/wiki/SMART-1" title="SMART-1">SMART-1</a> on 3 September 2006.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">Indian Space Research Organisation</a> (ISRO) performed a controlled crash impact with its <a href="/wiki/Moon_Impact_Probe" title="Moon Impact Probe">Moon Impact Probe</a> (MIP) on 14 November 2008. The MIP was an ejected probe from the Indian <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-1" title="Chandrayaan-1">Chandrayaan-1</a> lunar orbiter and performed <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a> experiments during its descent to the lunar surface.\n</p><p>The Chinese lunar orbiter <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_1" title="Chang\'e 1">Chang\'e 1</a> executed a controlled crash onto the surface of the Moon on 1 March 2009. The rover mission <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_3" title="Chang\'e 3">Chang\'e 3</a> soft-landed on 14 December 2013, as did its successor, <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_4" title="Chang\'e 4">Chang\'e 4</a>, on 3 January 2019. All manned and unmanned <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_landing_(rocketry)" title="Soft landing (rocketry)">soft landings</a> had taken place on the <a href="/wiki/Near_side_of_the_Moon" title="Near side of the Moon">near side of the Moon</a>, until 3 January 2019 when the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_4" title="Chang\'e 4">Chang\'e 4</a> spacecraft made the first landing on the <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">far side of the Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-far_side_of_the_moon_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-far_side_of_the_moon-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Manned_landings">Manned landings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Manned landings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing,_Apollo_16.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2369" data-file-width="2340" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg/220px-Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg/330px-Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg/440px-Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing%2C_Apollo_16.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_surface_shortly_after_landing,_Apollo_16.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The view through the window of the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">Lunar Module</a> <i>Orion</i> shortly after <a href="/wiki/Apollo_16" title="Apollo 16">Apollo 16</a>\'s landing.</div></div></div>\n<p>A total of twelve men have landed on the Moon. This was accomplished with two US pilot-astronauts flying a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Module" title="Lunar Module">Lunar Module</a> on each of six <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> missions across a 41-month period starting on 20 July 1969 UTC, with <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Buzz Aldrin</a> on <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a>, and ending on 14 December 1972 UTC with <a href="/wiki/Gene_Cernan" title="Gene Cernan">Gene Cernan</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jack_Schmitt" title="Jack Schmitt">Jack Schmitt</a> on <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">Apollo 17</a>. Cernan was the last to step off the lunar surface.\n</p><p>All Apollo lunar missions had a third crew member who remained on board the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Command_Module" title="Command Module">Command Module</a>. The last three missions had a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Roving_Vehicle" title="Lunar Roving Vehicle">rover</a> for increased mobility.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_background">Scientific background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Scientific background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In order to go to the Moon, a spacecraft must first leave the <a href="/wiki/Gravity_well" title="Gravity well">gravity well</a> of the Earth. The only practical way of accomplishing this currently is with a <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocket</a>. Unlike other airborne vehicles such as <a href="/wiki/Balloon_(aeronautics)" title="Balloon (aeronautics)">balloons</a> or <a href="/wiki/Jet_aircraft" title="Jet aircraft">jets</a>, a rocket is the only known form of propulsion which can continue to increase its <a href="/wiki/Speed" title="Speed">speed</a> at high altitudes in the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> outside the Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.\n</p><p>Upon approach of the target moon, a spacecraft will be drawn ever closer to its surface at increasing speeds due to gravity. In order to land intact it must decelerate to less than about 160 kilometres per hour (99&#160;mph) and be ruggedized to withstand a "hard landing" impact, or it must decelerate to negligible speed at contact for a "soft landing" (which is the only viable option with human occupants). The first three attempts by the U.S. to perform a successful hard Moon landing with a ruggedized <a href="/wiki/Seismometer" title="Seismometer">seismometer</a> package in 1962 all failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger3_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger3-6">[6]</a></sup> The Soviets first achieved the milestone of a hard lunar landing with a ruggedized camera in 1966, followed only months later by the first unmanned soft lunar landing by the U.S.\n</p><p>The speed of a crash landing on its surface is typically between 70 and 100% of the <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> of the target moon, and thus this is the total velocity which must be shed from the target moon\'s gravitational attraction for a soft landing to occur. For Earth\'s Moon, the escape velocity is 2.38 kilometres per second (1.48&#160;mi/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GgLunarXPrize-MoonInfo_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-GgLunarXPrize-MoonInfo-7">[7]</a></sup> The change in velocity (referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">delta-v</a>) is usually provided by a landing rocket, which must be carried into space by the original <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicle</a> as part of the overall spacecraft. An exception is the soft moon landing on <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> carried out by the <a href="/wiki/Huygens_(spacecraft)" title="Huygens (spacecraft)"><i>Huygens</i> probe</a> in 2005. As the moon with the thickest atmosphere, landings on Titan may be accomplished by using <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">atmospheric entry</a> techniques that are generally lighter in weight than a rocket with equivalent capability.\n</p><p>The Soviets succeeded in making the first crash landing on the Moon in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-luna2_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-luna2-8">[8]</a></sup>  Crash landings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hard-landing_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-hard-landing-9">[9]</a></sup> may occur because of malfunctions in a spacecraft, or they can be deliberately arranged for vehicles which do not have an onboard landing rocket. There have been <a href="/wiki/List_of_artificial_objects_on_the_Moon" title="List of artificial objects on the Moon">many such Moon crashes</a>, often with their flight path controlled to impact at precise locations on the lunar surface. For example, during the Apollo program the <a href="/wiki/S-IVB" title="S-IVB">S-IVB</a> third stage of the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> moon rocket as well as the spent ascent stage of the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">Lunar Module</a> were deliberately crashed on the Moon several times to provide impacts registering as a <a href="/wiki/Quake_(natural_phenomenon)#Moonquake" title="Quake (natural phenomenon)">moonquake</a> on <a href="/wiki/Seismometer" title="Seismometer">seismometers</a> that had been left on the lunar surface. Such crashes were instrumental in mapping the <a href="/wiki/Internal_structure_of_the_Moon" title="Internal structure of the Moon">internal structure of the Moon</a>.\n</p><p>To return to Earth, the escape velocity of the Moon must be overcome for the spacecraft to escape the <a href="/wiki/Gravity_well" title="Gravity well">gravity well</a> of the Moon. Rockets must be used to leave the Moon and return to space. Upon reaching Earth, atmospheric entry techniques are used to absorb the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of a returning spacecraft and reduce its speed for safe landing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> These functions greatly complicate a moon landing mission and lead to many additional operational considerations. Any moon departure rocket must first be carried to the Moon\'s surface by a moon landing rocket, increasing the latter\'s required size. The Moon departure rocket, larger moon landing rocket and any Earth atmosphere entry equipment such as heat shields and <a href="/wiki/Parachute" title="Parachute">parachutes</a> must in turn be lifted by the original launch vehicle, greatly increasing its size by a significant and almost prohibitive degree.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_background">Political background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Political background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a></div>\n<table class="box-Tone plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-Tone" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Moon+landing%22" rel="nofollow">"Moon landing"</a>&#160;&#8211;&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Moon+landing%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">news</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Moon+landing%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Moon+landing%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Moon+landing%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Moon+landing%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2013</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The intense efforts devoted in the 1960s to achieving first an unmanned and then ultimately a manned moon landing become easier to understand in the political context of its historical era. <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>  had introduced many new and deadly innovations including <a href="/wiki/Blitzkrieg" title="Blitzkrieg">blitzkrieg</a>-style surprise attacks used in the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">invasion of Poland</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attack on Pearl Harbor</a>; the <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">V-2 rocket</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile" title="Ballistic missile">ballistic missile</a> which killed thousands in attacks on London and <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a>; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atom_bomb" title="Atom bomb">atom bomb</a>, which killed hundreds of thousands in the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a>. In the 1950s, tensions mounted between the two ideologically opposed superpowers of the United States and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> that had emerged as victors in the conflict, particularly after the development by both countries of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bomb</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luna_3_moon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Luna_3_moon.jpg/220px-Luna_3_moon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Luna_3_moon.jpg/330px-Luna_3_moon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Luna_3_moon.jpg/440px-Luna_3_moon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luna_3_moon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first image of another world from space, returned by Luna 3, showed the far side of the Moon in October 1959.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Willy_Ley" title="Willy Ley">Willy Ley</a> wrote in 1957 that a rocket to the Moon "could be built later this year if somebody can be found to sign some papers".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ley195707_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-ley195707-10">[10]</a></sup> On 4 October 1957, the Soviet Union <a href="/wiki/Rocket_launch" title="Rocket launch">launched</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a></i> as the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_satellite" title="Artificial satellite">artificial satellite</a> to orbit the Earth and so initiated the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a>. This unexpected event was a source of pride to the Soviets and shock to the U.S., who could now potentially be surprise attacked by nuclear-tipped Soviet rockets in under 30 minutes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>   Also, the steady beeping of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_beacon" title="Radio beacon">radio beacon</a> aboard <i>Sputnik 1</i> as it passed overhead every 96 minutes was widely viewed on both sides<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  as effective propaganda to <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a> countries demonstrating the technological superiority of the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Political_system" title="Political system">political system</a> compared to that of the U.S. This perception was reinforced by a string of subsequent rapid-fire Soviet space achievements. In 1959, the R-7 rocket was used to launch the first escape from Earth\'s gravity into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_orbit" title="Solar orbit">solar orbit</a>, the first crash impact onto the surface of the Moon, and the first photography of the never-before-seen <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">far side of the Moon</a>. These were the <a href="/wiki/Luna_1" title="Luna 1">Luna 1</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luna_2" title="Luna 2">Luna 2</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Luna_3" title="Luna 3">Luna 3</a> spacecraft.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_Lander_Model.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2097" data-file-width="2697" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lunar_Lander_Model.jpg/220px-Lunar_Lander_Model.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lunar_Lander_Model.jpg/330px-Lunar_Lander_Model.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lunar_Lander_Model.jpg/440px-Lunar_Lander_Model.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunar_Lander_Model.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1963 conceptual model of the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">Apollo Lunar Excursion Module</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The U.S. response to these Soviet achievements was to greatly accelerate previously existing military space and missile projects and to create a civilian space agency, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>. Military efforts were initiated to develop and produce mass quantities of intercontinental ballistic missiles (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICBMs" title="ICBMs">ICBMs</a>) that would bridge the so-called <a href="/wiki/Missile_gap" title="Missile gap">missile gap</a> and enable a policy of <a href="/wiki/Deterrence_theory" title="Deterrence theory">deterrence</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warfare" title="Nuclear warfare">nuclear war</a> with the Soviets known as <a href="/wiki/Mutual_assured_destruction" title="Mutual assured destruction">mutual assured destruction</a> or MAD. These newly developed <a href="/wiki/Missile" title="Missile">missiles</a> were made available to civilians of NASA for various projects (which would have the added benefit of demonstrating the payload, guidance accuracy and reliabilities of U.S. ICBMs to the Soviets).\n</p><p>While NASA stressed peaceful and scientific uses for these rockets, their use in various lunar exploration efforts also had secondary goal of realistic, goal-oriented testing of the missiles themselves and development of associated infrastructure,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> just as the Soviets were doing with their R-7.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Early_Soviet_unmanned_lunar_missions_.281958.E2.80.931965.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Soviet_unmanned_lunar_missions_(1958&#8211;1965)">Early Soviet unmanned lunar missions (1958&#8211;1965)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early Soviet unmanned lunar missions (1958&#8211;1965)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunik_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="448" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Lunik_3.jpg/170px-Lunik_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Lunik_3.jpg/255px-Lunik_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Lunik_3.jpg/340px-Lunik_3.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunik_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Luna 3 transmitted the first photos of the far side of the Moon</div></div></div>\n<p>After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Fall of the Soviet Union">fall of the Soviet Union</a> in 1991, historical records were released to allow the true accounting of Soviet lunar efforts. Unlike the U.S. tradition of assigning a particular mission name in advance of a launch, the Soviets assigned a public "<a href="/wiki/Luna_programme" title="Luna programme">Luna</a>" mission number only if a launch resulted in a spacecraft going beyond Earth orbit. The policy had the effect of hiding Soviet Moon mission failures from public view. If the attempt failed in Earth orbit before departing for the Moon, it was frequently (but not always) given a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sputnik_programme" title="Sputnik programme">Sputnik</a>" or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmos_(satellite)" title="Cosmos (satellite)">Cosmos</a>" Earth-orbit mission number to hide its purpose. Launch explosions were not acknowledged at all.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="background: #f9f9f9; border-collapse: collapse;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th align="left">Mission\n</th>\n<th align="right">3 December 1965\n</td>\n<td>Landing\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; lunar impact during landing attempt\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<h2><span id="Early_U.S._unmanned_lunar_missions_.281958.E2.80.931965.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_U.S._unmanned_lunar_missions_(1958&#8211;1965)">Early U.S. unmanned lunar missions (1958&#8211;1965)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early U.S. unmanned lunar missions (1958&#8211;1965)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ranger_9_sudden_impact.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="589" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Ranger_9_sudden_impact.gif/220px-Ranger_9_sudden_impact.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Ranger_9_sudden_impact.gif/330px-Ranger_9_sudden_impact.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Ranger_9_sudden_impact.gif/440px-Ranger_9_sudden_impact.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ranger_9_sudden_impact.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist\'s portrayal of a Ranger spacecraft right before impact</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ranger8-moon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="675" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Ranger8-moon.jpg/220px-Ranger8-moon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Ranger8-moon.jpg/330px-Ranger8-moon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Ranger8-moon.jpg/440px-Ranger8-moon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ranger8-moon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the last photos of the Moon transmitted by Ranger 8 right before impact</div></div></div>\n<p>In contrast to Soviet lunar exploration triumphs in 1959, success eluded initial U.S. efforts to reach the Moon with the <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_program" title="Pioneer program">Pioneer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ranger_program" title="Ranger program">Ranger programs</a>. Fifteen consecutive U.S. unmanned lunar missions over a six-year period from 1958 to 1964 all failed their primary photographic missions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> however, Rangers 4 and 6 successfully repeated the Soviet lunar impacts as part of their secondary missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger4_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger4-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger6_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger6-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Failures included three U.S. attempts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger3_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger3-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger4_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger4-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger5_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger5-15">[15]</a></sup> in 1962 to hard land small seismometer packages released by the main Ranger spacecraft. These surface packages were to use <a href="/wiki/Retrorocket" title="Retrorocket">retrorockets</a> to survive landing, unlike the parent vehicle, which was designed to deliberately crash onto the surface. The final three Ranger probes performed successful high altitude lunar <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnaissance</a>  photography missions during intentional crash impacts between 2.62 and 2.68 kilometres per second (9,400 and 9,600&#160;km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger7_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger7-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger8_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger8-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger9_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger9-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="background: #f9f9f9; border-collapse: collapse;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th align="left">Mission\n</th>\n<th align="center">Mass (kg)\n</th>\n<th align="center">Launch vehicle\n</th>\n<th align="center">Launch date\n</th>\n<th align="center">Goal\n</th>\n<th align="center">Result\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_0" title="Pioneer 0">Pioneer 0</a>\n</td>\n<td>38\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Thor-Able" title="Thor-Able">Thor-Able</a>\n</td>\n<td align="right">17 August 1958\n</td>\n<td>Lunar orbit\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; first stage explosion; destroyed\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_1" title="Pioneer 1">Pioneer 1</a>\n</td>\n<td>34\n</td>\n<td>Thor-Able\n</td>\n<td align="right">11 October 1958\n</td>\n<td>Lunar orbit\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; software error; reentry\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_2" title="Pioneer 2">Pioneer 2</a>\n</td>\n<td>39\n</td>\n<td>Thor-Able\n</td>\n<td align="right">8 November 1958\n</td>\n<td>Lunar orbit\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; third stage misfire; reentry\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_3" title="Pioneer 3">Pioneer 3</a>\n</td>\n<td>6\n</td>\n<td>Juno\n</td>\n<td align="right">6 December 1958\n</td>\n<td>Flyby\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; pad explosion; destroyed\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_P-3" title="Pioneer P-3">Pioneer P-3</a>\n</td>\n<td>168\n</td>\n<td>Atlas-Able\n</td>\n<td align="right">29 November 1959\n</td>\n<td>Lunar orbit\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; upper stage anomaly; reentry\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ranger_2" title="Ranger 2">Ranger 2</a>\n</td>\n<td>304\n</td>\n<td>Atlas &#8211; Agena\n</td>\n<td align="right">18 November 1961\n</td>\n<td>Prototype test\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; booster guidance; solar orbit\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ranger_4" title="Ranger 4">Ranger 4</a>\n</td>\n<td>331\n</td>\n<td>Atlas &#8211; Agena\n</td>\n<td align="right">23 April 1962\n</td>\n<td>Landing\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:orange;">Partial success</span> &#8211; first U.S. spacecraft to reach another celestial body; crash impact &#8211; no photos returned\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ranger_5" title="Ranger 5">Ranger 5</a>\n</td>\n<td>342\n</td>\n<td>Atlas &#8211; Agena\n</td>\n<td align="right">18 October 1962\n</td>\n<td>Landing\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; spacecraft power; solar orbit\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ranger_6" title="Ranger 6">Ranger 6</a>\n</td>\n<td>367\n</td>\n<td>Atlas &#8211; Agena\n</td>\n<td align="right">17 February 1965\n</td>\n<td>Impact\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:green;">Success</span> &#8211; returned 7137 photos, crash impact\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ranger_9" title="Ranger 9">Ranger 9</a>\n</td>\n<td>367\n</td>\n<td>Atlas &#8211; Agena\n</td>\n<td align="right">21 March 1965\n</td>\n<td>Impact\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:green;">Success</span> &#8211; returned 5814 photos, crash impact\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pioneer_missions">Pioneer missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Pioneer missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Three different designs of Pioneer lunar probes were flown on three different modified ICBMs. Those flown on the <a href="/wiki/PGM-17_Thor" title="PGM-17 Thor">Thor</a> booster modified with an Able upper stage carried an <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> image scanning television system with a <a href="/wiki/Image_resolution" title="Image resolution">resolution</a> of 1 <a href="/wiki/Milliradian" title="Milliradian">milliradian</a> to study the Moon\'s surface, an <a href="/wiki/Ionization_chamber" title="Ionization chamber">ionization chamber</a> to measure <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> in space, a diaphragm/microphone assembly to detect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrometeorites" title="Micrometeorites">micrometeorites</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometer</a>, and temperature-variable resistors to monitor spacecraft internal thermal conditions. The first, a mission managed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a>, exploded during launch; all subsequent Pioneer lunar flights had NASA as the lead management organization. The next two returned to Earth and burned up upon reentry into the atmosphere after achieved maximum altitudes of around 110,000 kilometres (68,000&#160;mi) and 1,450 kilometres (900&#160;mi), far short of the roughly 400,000 kilometres (250,000&#160;mi) required to reach the vicinity of the Moon.\n</p><p>NASA then collaborated with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Army_Ballistic_Missile_Agency" title="Army Ballistic Missile Agency">Ballistic Missile Agency</a> to fly two extremely small cone-shaped probes on the <a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a> ICBM, carrying only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photocells" title="Photocells">photocells</a> which would be triggered by the light of the Moon and a lunar radiation environment experiment using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geiger-M%C3%BCller_tube" title="Geiger-M&#252;ller tube">Geiger-M&#252;ller tube</a> detector. The first of these reached an altitude of only around 100,000 kilometres (62,000&#160;mi), serendipitously gathering data that established the presence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_Allen_radiation_belts" title="Van Allen radiation belts">Van Allen radiation belts</a> before reentering Earth\'s atmosphere. The second passed by the Moon at a distance of more than 60,000 kilometres (37,000&#160;mi), twice as far as planned and too far away to trigger either of the on-board scientific instruments, yet still becoming the first U.S. spacecraft to reach a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_orbit" title="Solar orbit">solar orbit</a>.\n</p><p>The final Pioneer lunar probe design consisted of four "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paddlewheel" title="Paddlewheel">paddlewheel</a>" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_module" title="Photovoltaic module">solar panels</a> extending from a one-meter diameter spherical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-stabilized_satellite" title="Spin-stabilized satellite">spin-stabilized</a> spacecraft body that was equipped to take images of the lunar surface with a television-like system, estimate the Moon\'s mass and topography of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peak_of_Eternal_Light" title="Peak of Eternal Light">poles</a>, record the distribution and velocity of micrometeorites, study radiation, measure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_field_of_celestial_bodies" title="Magnetic field of celestial bodies">magnetic fields</a>, detect <a href="/wiki/Alfv%C3%A9n_wave" title="Alfv&#233;n wave">low frequency electromagnetic waves</a> in space and use a sophisticated integrated <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_propulsion" title="Spacecraft propulsion">propulsion</a> system for maneuvering and orbit insertion as well. None of the four spacecraft built in this series of probes survived launch on its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlas_(missile)" title="Atlas (missile)">Atlas</a> ICBM outfitted with an Able upper stage.\n</p><p>Following the unsuccessful Atlas-Able Pioneer probes, NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a> embarked upon an unmanned spacecraft development program whose modular design could be used to support both lunar and interplanetary exploration missions. The interplanetary versions were known as <a href="/wiki/Mariner_program" title="Mariner program">Mariners</a>; lunar versions were <a href="/wiki/Ranger_program" title="Ranger program">Rangers</a>. JPL envisioned three versions of the Ranger lunar probes: Block I prototypes, which would carry various radiation detectors in test flights to a very high Earth orbit that came nowhere near the Moon; Block II, which would try to accomplish the first Moon landing by hard landing a seismometer package; and Block III, which would crash onto the lunar surface without any braking rockets while taking very high resolution wide-area photographs of the Moon during their descent.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ranger_missions">Ranger missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ranger missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ranger_program" title="Ranger program">Ranger program</a></div>\n<p>The Ranger 1 and 2 Block I missions were virtually identical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger1_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger1-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger2_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger2-20">[20]</a></sup> Spacecraft experiments included a <a href="/wiki/H-alpha" title="H-alpha">Lyman-alpha</a> telescope, a <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">rubidium-vapor</a> <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometer</a>, electrostatic analyzers, medium-energy-range <a href="/wiki/Particle_detector" title="Particle detector">particle detectors</a>, two triple coincidence telescopes, a cosmic-ray integrating <a href="/wiki/Ionization_chamber" title="Ionization chamber">ionization chamber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a> detectors, and <a href="/wiki/Scintillation_counter" title="Scintillation counter">scintillation counters</a>. The goal was to place these Block I spacecraft in a very high Earth orbit with an apogee of 110,000 kilometres (68,000&#160;mi) and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perigee" title="Perigee">perigee</a> of 60,000 kilometres (37,000&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger1_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger1-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From that vantage point, scientists could make direct measurements of the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> over a period of many months while engineers perfected new methods to routinely track and communicate with spacecraft over such large distances. Such practice was deemed vital to be assured of capturing high-bandwidth television transmissions from the Moon during a one-shot fifteen-minute time window in subsequent Block II and Block III lunar descents. Both Block I missions suffered failures of the new Agena upper stage and never left low Earth <a href="/wiki/Parking_orbit" title="Parking orbit">parking orbit</a> after launch; both burned up upon reentry after only a few days.\n</p><p>The first attempts to perform a Moon landing took place in 1962 during the Rangers 3, 4 and 5 missions flown by the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger3_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger3-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger4_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger4-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger5_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger5-15">[15]</a></sup> All three Block II missions basic vehicles were 3.1&#160;m high and consisted of a lunar capsule covered with a balsa wood impact-limiter, 650&#160;mm in diameter, a mono-propellant mid-course motor, a retrorocket with a thrust of 5,050 pounds-force (22.5&#160;kN),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger4_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger4-13">[13]</a></sup> and a gold- and chrome-plated hexagonal base 1.5&#160;m in diameter. This lander (code-named <i>Tonto</i>) was designed to provide impact cushioning using an exterior blanket of crushable balsa wood and an interior filled with incompressible liquid <a href="/wiki/Freon" title="Freon">freon</a>. A 42&#160;kg (56 pounds) 30-centimetre-diameter (0.98&#160;ft) metal payload sphere floated and was free to rotate in a liquid freon reservoir contained in the landing sphere<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.\n</p><p>This payload sphere contained six silver-<a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> batteries to power a fifty-milliwatt radio transmitter, a temperature sensitive voltage controlled oscillator to measure lunar surface temperatures, and a seismometer that was designed with sensitivity high enough to detect the impact of a 5&#160;lb (2.3&#160;kg) meteorite on the opposite side of the Moon. Weight was distributed in the payload sphere so it would rotate in its liquid blanket to place the seismometer into an upright and operational position no matter what the final resting orientation of the external landing sphere. After landing, plugs were to be opened allowing the freon to evaporate and the payload sphere to settle into upright contact with the landing sphere. The batteries were sized to allow up to three months of operation for the payload sphere. Various mission constraints limited the landing site to Oceanus Procellarum on the lunar equator, which the lander ideally would reach 66 hours after launch.\n</p><p>No cameras were carried by the Ranger landers, and no pictures were to be captured from the lunar surface during the mission. Instead, the 3.1 metres (10&#160;ft) Ranger Block II mother ship carried a 200-scan-line television camera which was to capture images during the free-fall descent to the lunar surface. The camera was designed to transmit a picture every 10 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa-ranger4_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-nasa-ranger4-13">[13]</a></sup> Seconds before impact, at 5 and 0.6 kilometres (3.11 and 0.37&#160;mi) above the lunar surface, the Ranger mother ships took picture (which may be viewed <a class="external text" href="http://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/planetary/lunar/ranger.html" rel="nofollow">here</a>).\n</p><p>Other instruments gathering data before the mother ship crashed onto the Moon were a gamma ray spectrometer to measure overall lunar chemical composition and a radar altimeter. The radar altimeter was to give a signal ejecting the landing capsule and its solid-fueled braking rocket overboard from the Block II mother ship. The braking rocket was to slow and the landing sphere to a dead stop at 330 metres (1,080&#160;ft) above the surface and separate, allowing the landing sphere to free fall once more and hit the surface<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.\n</p><p>On Ranger 3, failure of the Atlas guidance system and a software error aboard the Agena upper stage combined to put the spacecraft on a course that would miss the Moon. Attempts to salvage lunar photography during a flyby of the Moon were thwarted by in-flight failure of the onboard flight computer. This was probably because of prior <a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(microbiology)" title="Sterilization (microbiology)">heat sterilization</a> of the spacecraft by keeping it above the <a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">boiling</a> point of water for 24 hours on the ground, to protect the Moon from being contaminated by Earth organisms. Heat sterilization was also blamed for subsequent in-flight failures of the spacecraft computer on Ranger 4 and the power subsystem on Ranger 5. Only Ranger 4 reached the Moon in an uncontrolled crash impact on the far side of the Moon.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Heat sterilization was discontinued for the final four Block III Ranger probes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> These replaced the Block II landing capsule and its retrorocket with a heavier, more capable television system to support landing site selection for upcoming Apollo manned Moon landing missions. Six cameras were designed to take thousands of high-altitude photographs in the final twenty-minute period before crashing on the lunar surface. Camera resolution was 1,132 scan lines, far higher than the 525 lines found in a typical U.S. 1964 home television. While <a href="/wiki/Ranger_6" title="Ranger 6">Ranger 6</a> suffered a failure of this camera system and returned no photographs despite an otherwise successful flight, the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Ranger_7" title="Ranger 7">Ranger 7</a> mission to Mare Cognitum was a complete success.\n</p><p>Breaking the six-year string of failures in U.S. attempts to photograph the Moon at close range, the <a href="/wiki/Ranger_7" title="Ranger 7">Ranger 7</a> mission was viewed as a national turning point and instrumental in allowing the key 1965 NASA budget appropriation to pass through the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> intact without a reduction in funds for the Apollo manned Moon landing program. Subsequent successes with <a href="/wiki/Ranger_8" title="Ranger 8">Ranger 8</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ranger_9" title="Ranger 9">Ranger 9</a> further buoyed U.S. hopes.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Soviet_unmanned_soft_landings_.281966.E2.80.931976.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_unmanned_soft_landings_(1966&#8211;1976)">Soviet unmanned soft landings (1966&#8211;1976)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Soviet unmanned soft landings (1966&#8211;1976)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9B%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0-16.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3135" data-file-width="2644" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/%D0%9B%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0-16.jpg/170px-%D0%9B%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0-16.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/%D0%9B%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0-16.jpg/255px-%D0%9B%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0-16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/%D0%9B%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0-16.jpg/340px-%D0%9B%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0-16.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9B%D1%83%D0%BD%D0%B0-16.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Model of Luna 16 Moon soil sample return lander</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soviet_moonrover.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Soviet_moonrover.JPG/220px-Soviet_moonrover.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Soviet_moonrover.JPG/330px-Soviet_moonrover.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Soviet_moonrover.JPG/440px-Soviet_moonrover.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soviet_moonrover.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Model of Soviet Lunokhod automatic moon rover</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Luna_9" title="Luna 9">Luna 9</a> spacecraft, launched by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, performed the first successful soft Moon landing on 3 February, 1966. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airbags#Airbag_landing_systems" title="Airbags">Airbags</a> protected its 99 kilograms (218&#160;lb) ejectable capsule which survived an impact speed of over 15 metres per second (54&#160;km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronautix-luna9_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-astronautix-luna9-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Luna_13" title="Luna 13">Luna 13</a> duplicated this feat with a similar Moon landing on 24 December 1966. Both returned panoramic photographs that were the first views from the lunar surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Luna_16" title="Luna 16">Luna 16</a> was the first <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">robotic probe</a> to land on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> and safely return a sample of lunar soil back to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> It represented the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_sample" title="Lunar sample">lunar sample</a> return mission by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, and was the third lunar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_return_mission" title="Sample return mission">sample return mission</a> overall, following the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">Apollo 12</a> missions. This mission was later successfully repeated by <a href="/wiki/Luna_20" title="Luna 20">Luna 20</a> (1972) and <a href="/wiki/Luna_24" title="Luna 24">Luna 24</a> (1976).\n</p><p>In 1970 and 1973 two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunokhod" title="Lunokhod">Lunokhod</a> ("Moonwalker") robotic lunar rovers were delivered to the Moon, where they successfully operated for 10 and 4 months respectively, covering 10.5&#160;km (<a href="/wiki/Lunokhod_1" title="Lunokhod 1">Lunokhod 1</a>) and 37&#160;km (<a href="/wiki/Lunokhod_2" title="Lunokhod 2">Lunokhod 2</a>). These rover missions were in operation concurrently with the Zond and Luna series of Moon flyby, orbiter and landing missions.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="background: #f9f9f9; border-collapse: collapse;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th align="left">Mission\n</th>\n<th align="center">Mass (kg)\n</th>\n<th align="center">Booster\n</th>\n<th align="center">Launch date\n</th>\n<th align="center">Goal\n</th>\n<th align="center">Result\n</th>\n<th align="center">Landing zone\n</th>\n<th align="center"><a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">Lat</a>/<a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">Lon</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Luna_9" title="Luna 9">Luna-9</a>\n</td>\n<td>1580\n</td>\n<td>Semyorka &#8211; 8K78\n</td>\n<td align="right">31 January id="U.S._unmanned_soft_landings_.281966.E2.80.931968.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._unmanned_soft_landings_(1966&#8211;1968)">U.S. unmanned soft landings (1966&#8211;1968)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: U.S. unmanned soft landings (1966&#8211;1968)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Surveyor_1_launch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="275" href="/wiki/File:Surveyor_1_launch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Launch of Surveyor 1.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Surveyor_3-Apollo_12.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2627" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="193" 12</a>, stands next to Surveyor 3 lander. In the background is the Apollo 12 lander, <i>Intrepid</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The U.S. <a href="/wiki/Robot" title="Robot">robotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Surveyor_program" title="Surveyor program">Surveyor program</a> was part of an effort to locate a safe site on the Moon for a human landing and test under lunar conditions the <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> and landing systems required to make a true controlled touchdown. Five of Surveyor\'s seven missions made successful unmanned Moon landings. Surveyor 3 was visited two years after its Moon landing by the crew of Apollo 12. They removed parts of it for examination back on Earth to determine the effects of long-term exposure to the lunar environment.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="background: #f9f9f9; border-collapse: collapse;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th align="left">Mission\n</th>\n<th align="center">Mass (kg)\n</th>\n<th align="center">Booster\n</th>\n<th align="center">Launch date\n</th>\n<th align="center">Goal\n</th>\n<th align="center">Result\n</th>\n<th align="center">Landing zone\n</th>\n<th align="center"><a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">Lat</a>/<a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">Lon</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Surveyor_1" title="Surveyor 1">Surveyor 1</a>\n</td>\n<td>292\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlas_(rocket)" title="Atlas (rocket)">Atlas</a> &#8211; To the general public both countries had demonstrated roughly equal technical capabilities by returning photographic images from the surface of the Moon. These pictures provided a key affirmative answer to the crucial question of whether or not lunar soil would support upcoming manned landers with their much greater weight.\n</p><p>However, the Luna 9 hard landing of a ruggedized sphere using airbags at a 50 kilometres (31&#160;mi)-per-hour ballistic impact speed had much more in common with the failed 1962 Ranger landing attempts and their planned 160 kilometres (99&#160;mi)-per-hour impacts than with the Surveyor 1 soft landing on three footpads using its radar-controlled, adjustable-thrust retrorocket. While Luna 9 and Surveyor 1 were both major national accomplishments, only Surveyor 1 had reached its landing site employing key technologies that would be needed for a manned flight. Thus as of mid-1966, the United States had begun to pull ahead of the Soviet Union in the so-called Space Race to land a man on the Moon.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Space_Race_1957-1975_black_text.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1350" decoding="async" height="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Space_Race_1957-1975_black_text.png/450px-Space_Race_1957-1975_black_text.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Space_Race_1957-1975_black_text.png/675px-Space_Race_1957-1975_black_text.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Space_Race_1957-1975_black_text.png/900px-Space_Race_1957-1975_black_text.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Space_Race_1957-1975_black_text.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A timeline of the space race between 1957 and 1975, with missions from the US and USSR.</div></div></div>\n<p>Advances in other areas were necessary before manned spacecraft could follow unmanned ones to the surface of the Moon. Of particular importance was developing the expertise to perform flight operations in lunar orbit. Ranger, Surveyor and initial Luna Moon landing attempts all utilized flight paths from Earth that traveled directly to the lunar surface without first placing the spacecraft in a lunar orbit. Such <a href="/wiki/Direct_ascent" title="Direct ascent">direct ascents</a> use a minimum amount of fuel for unmanned spacecraft on a one-way trip.\n</p><p>In contrast, manned vehicles need additional fuel after a lunar landing to enable a return trip back to Earth for the crew. Leaving this massive amount of required Earth-return fuel in lunar orbit until it is used later in the mission is far more efficient than taking such fuel down to the lunar surface in a Moon landing and then hauling it all back into space yet again, working against lunar gravity both ways. Such considerations lead logically to a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_orbit_rendezvous" title="Lunar orbit rendezvous">lunar orbit rendezvous</a> mission profile for a manned Moon landing.\n</p><p>Accordingly, beginning in mid-1966 both the U.S. and U.S.S.R. naturally progressed into missions which featured lunar orbit operations as a necessary prerequisite to a manned Moon landing. The primary goals of these initial unmanned orbiters were extensive photographic mapping of the entire lunar surface for the selection of manned landing sites and, for the Soviets, the checkout of radio communications gear that would be used in future soft landings.\n</p><p>An unexpected major discovery from initial lunar orbiters were vast volumes of dense materials beneath the surface of the Moon\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_maria" title="Lunar maria">maria</a>. Such mass concentrations ("<a href="/wiki/Mass_concentration_(astronomy)" title="Mass concentration (astronomy)">mascons</a>") can send a manned mission dangerously off course in the final minutes of a Moon landing when aiming for a relatively small landing zone that is smooth and safe. Mascons were also found over a longer period of time to greatly disturb the orbits of low-altitude satellites around the Moon, making their orbits unstable and forcing an inevitable crash on the lunar surface in the relatively short period of months to a few years.\n</p><p>Controlling the location of impact for spent lunar orbiters can have scientific value. For example, in 1999 the NASA <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Prospector" title="Lunar Prospector">Lunar Prospector</a> orbiter was deliberately targeted to impact a permanently shadowed area of Shoemaker Crater near the lunar south pole. It was hoped that energy from the impact would vaporize suspected shadowed ice deposits in the crater and liberate a water vapor plume that would be detectable from Earth. No such plume was observed. However, a small vial of ashes from the body of pioneer lunar scientist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugene_Shoemaker" title="Eugene Shoemaker">Eugene Shoemaker</a> was delivered by the Lunar Prospector to the crater named in his honor &#8211; currently the only human remains on the Moon.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Soviet_lunar_orbit_satellites_.281966.E2.80.931974.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_lunar_orbit_satellites_(1966&#8211;1974)">Soviet lunar orbit satellites (1966&#8211;1974)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Soviet lunar orbit satellites (1966&#8211;1974)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable" style="background: #f9f9f9; border-collapse: collapse;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th align="center">U.S.S.R. mission\n</th>\n<th href="/wiki/Luna_10" title="Luna 10">Luna 10</a> became the first spacecraft to orbit the Moon on 3 April 1966.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="U.S._lunar_orbit_satellites_.281966.E2.80.931967.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._lunar_orbit_satellites_(1966&#8211;1967)">U.S. lunar orbit satellites (1966&#8211;1967)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: U.S. lunar orbit satellites (1966&#8211;1967)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable" style="background: #f9f9f9; border-collapse: collapse;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th align="left">U.S. mission\n</th>\n<th align="center">Mass (kg)\n</th>\n<th align="center">Booster\n</th>\n<th align="center">Launched\n</th>\n<th align="center">Mission goal\n</th>\n<th align="center">Mission result\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbiter_1" title="Lunar Orbiter 1">Lunar Orbiter 1</a>\n</td>\n<td>386\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlas_(missile)" title="Atlas (missile)">Atlas</a> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/RM-81_Agena" title="RM-81 Agena">Agena</a>\n</td>\n<td align="right">10 August 1966\n</td>\n<td>Lunar orbiter\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:green;">Success</span> &#8211; 1,160&#160;km X 189&#160;km x 12 deg orbit, 208&#160;m period, 80-day photography mission\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbiter_2" title="Lunar Orbiter 3">Lunar Orbiter 3</a>\n</td>\n<td>386\n</td>\n<td>Atlas &#8211; Agena\n</td>\n<td align="right">5 February 1967\n</td>\n<td>Lunar orbiter\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:green;">Success</span> &#8211; 1,860&#160;km X 52&#160;km x 21 deg orbit, 208&#160;m period, 246-day photography mission\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Lunar_Orbiter_4"
