title="Lunar Orbiter 5">Lunar Orbiter 5</a>\n</td>\n<td>386\n</td>\n<td>Atlas &#8211; Agena\n</td>\n<td align="right">1 August 1967\n</td>\n<td>Lunar orbiter\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:green;">Success</span> &#8211; 6,023&#160;km X 195&#160;km x 85 deg orbit, 510&#160;m period, 183-day photography mission\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<h2><span id="Soviet_circumlunar_loop_flights_.281967.E2.80.931970.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_circumlunar_loop_flights_(1967&#8211;1970)">Soviet circumlunar loop flights (1967&#8211;1970)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Soviet circumlunar loop flights (1967&#8211;1970)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_manned_lunar_programs" title="Soviet manned lunar programs">Soviet manned lunar programs</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zond_Assembly.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="448" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Zond_Assembly.jpg/250px-Zond_Assembly.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Zond_Assembly.jpg/375px-Zond_Assembly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/11/Zond_Assembly.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zond_Assembly.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zond_program" title="Zond program">Zond</a> mounted on top stage of Proton booster in assembly hangar.</div></div></div>\n<p>It is possible to aim a spacecraft from Earth so that it will loop around the Moon and return to Earth without entering lunar orbit, following the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_return_trajectory" title="Free return trajectory">free return trajectory</a>. Such circumlunar loop missions are simpler than lunar orbit missions because rockets for lunar orbit braking and Earth return are not required. However, a manned circumlunar loop trip poses significant challenges above and beyond those found in a manned low-Earth-orbit mission, offering valuable lessons in preparation for a manned Moon landing. Foremost among these are mastering the demands of re-entering the Earth\'s atmosphere upon returning from the Moon.\n</p><p>Manned Earth-orbiting vehicles such as the Space Shuttle return to Earth from speeds of around 17,000 miles per hour (27,000&#160;km/h; 7,600&#160;m/s). Due to the effects of gravity, a vehicle returning from the Moon hits Earth\'s atmosphere at a much higher speed of around 25,000 miles per hour (40,000&#160;km/h; 11,000&#160;m/s). The <a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">g-loading</a> on astronauts during the resulting <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">deceleration</a> can be at the limits of human endurance even during a nominal reentry. Slight variations in the vehicle flight path and reentry angle during a return from the Moon can easily result in fatal levels of deceleration force.\n</p><p>Achieving a manned circumlunar loop flight prior to a manned lunar landing became a primary goal of the Soviets with their <a href="/wiki/Zond_program" title="Zond program">Zond</a> spacecraft program. The first three Zonds were unmanned planetary probes; after that, the Zond name was transferred to a completely separate manned program. The initial focus of these later Zonds was extensive testing of required high-speed reentry techniques. This focus was not shared by the U.S., who chose instead to bypass the stepping stone of a manned circumlunar loop mission and never developed a separate spacecraft for this purpose.\n</p><p>Initial manned spaceflights in the early 1960s placed a single person in low Earth orbit during the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Vostok_programme" title="Vostok programme">Vostok</a> and U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercury_program" title="Mercury program">Mercury</a> programs. A two-flight extension of the Vostok program known as <a href="/wiki/Voskhod_programme" title="Voskhod programme">Voskhod</a> effectively used Vostok capsules with their ejection seats removed to achieve Soviet space firsts of multiple person crews in 1964 and spacewalks in early 1965. These capabilities were later demonstrated by the U.S. in ten <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gemini_program" title="Gemini program">Gemini</a> low Earth orbit missions throughout 1965 and 1966, using a totally new second-generation spacecraft design that had little in common with the earlier Mercury. These Gemini missions went on to prove critical techniques for orbital <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">rendezvous and docking</a> that were crucial to a manned lunar landing mission profile.\n</p><p>After the end of the Gemini program, the Soviets Union began flying their second-generation Zond manned spacecraft in 1967 with the ultimate goal of looping a cosmonaut around the Moon and returning him immediately to Earth. The <a href="/wiki/Zond_program" title="Zond program">Zond</a> spacecraft was launched with the simpler and already operational <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_rocket" title="Proton rocket">Proton</a> launch rocket, unlike the parallel Soviet manned Moon landing effort also underway at the time based on third-generation <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soyuz_spacecraft" title="Soyuz spacecraft">Soyuz spacecraft</a> requiring development of the advanced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-1_rocket" title="N-1 rocket">N-1</a> booster. The Soviets thus believed they could achieve a manned Zond circumlunar flight years before a U.S. manned lunar landing and so score a propaganda victory. However, significant development problems delayed the Zond program and the success of the U.S. Apollo lunar landing program led to the eventual termination of the Zond effort.\n</p><p>Like Zond, Apollo Moon flights were generally launched on a free return trajectory that would return them to Earth via a circumlunar loop in the event that a Service Module malfunction failed to place them in lunar orbit as planned. This option was implemented after an explosion aboard the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">Apollo 13</a> mission in 1970, which is the only manned circumlunar loop mission flown to date.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="background: #f9f9f9; border-collapse: collapse;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th align="left">U.S.S.R mission\n</th>\n<th align="center">Mass (kg)\n</th>\n<th align="center">Booster\n</th>\n<th align="center">Launched\n</th>\n<th align="center">Mission goal\n</th>\n<th align="center">Payload\n</th>\n<th align="center">Mission result\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cosmos-146&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cosmos-146 (page does not exist)">Cosmos-146</a>\n</td>\n<td>5,400\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_rocket" title="Proton rocket">Proton</a>\n</td>\n<td align="right">10 March 1967\n</td>\n<td>High Earth Orbit\n</td>\n<td>unmanned\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:orange;">Partial success</span> &#8211; Successfully reached high Earth orbit, but became stranded and was unable to initiate controlled high speed atmospheric reentry test\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cosmos-154&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cosmos-154 (page does not exist)">Cosmos-154</a>\n</td>\n<td>5,400\n</td>\n<td>Proton\n</td>\n<td align="right">8 April 1967\n</td>\n<td>High Earth Orbit\n</td>\n<td>unmanned\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:orange;">Partial success</span> &#8211; Successfully reached high Earth orbit, but became stranded and was unable to initiate controlled high speed atmospheric reentry test\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>Proton\n</td>\n<td align="right">28 September 1967\n</td>\n<td>High Earth Orbit\n</td>\n<td>unmanned\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; booster malfunction, failed to reach Earth orbit\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>Proton\n</td>\n<td align="right">22 November 1967\n</td>\n<td>High Earth Orbit\n</td>\n<td>unmanned\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; booster malfunction, failed to reach Earth orbit\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Zond_4" title="Zond 4">Zond-4</a>\n</td>\n<td>5,140\n</td>\n<td>Proton\n</td>\n<td align="right">2 March 1968\n</td>\n<td>High Earth Orbit\n</td>\n<td>unmanned\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:orange;">Partial success</span> &#8211; launched successfully to 300,000&#160;km high Earth orbit, high speed reentry test guidance malfunction, intentional self-destruct to prevent landfall outside Soviet Union\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>Proton\n</td>\n<td align="right">23 April 1968\n</td>\n<td>Circumlunar Loop\n</td>\n<td>non-human biological payload\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; booster malfunction, failed to reach Earth orbit; launch preparation tank explosion kills three in pad crew\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Zond_5" title="Zond 5">Zond-5</a>\n</td>\n<td>5,375\n</td>\n<td>Proton\n</td>\n<td align="right">15 September 1968\n</td>\n<td>Circumlunar Loop\n</td>\n<td>non-human biological payload\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:green;">Success</span> &#8211; looped around Moon, returned two tortoises and other live biological specimens and payload safely to Earth despite landing off-target outside the Soviet Union in the Indian Ocean\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Zond_6" title="Zond 6">Zond-6</a>\n</td>\n<td>5,375\n</td>\n<td>Proton\n</td>\n<td align="right">10 November 1968\n</td>\n<td>Circumlunar Loop\n</td>\n<td>non-human biological payload\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:orange;">Partial success</span> &#8211; looped around Moon, successful reentry, but loss of cabin air pressure caused biological payload death, parachute system malfunction and severe vehicle damage upon landing\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>Proton\n</td>\n<td align="right">20 January 1969\n</td>\n<td>Circumlunar Loop\n</td>\n<td>non-human biological payload\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:red;">Failure</span> &#8211; booster malfunction, failed to reach Earth orbit\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Zond_7" title="Zond 7">Zond-7</a>\n</td>\n<td>5,979\n</td>\n<td>Proton\n</td>\n<td align="right">8 August 1969\n</td>\n<td>Circumlunar Loop\n</td>\n<td>non-human biological payload\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:green;">Success</span> &#8211; looped around Moon, returned biological payload safely to Earth and landed on-target inside Soviet Union. Only Zond mission whose reentry G-forces would have been survivable by human crew had they been aboard.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Zond_8" title="Zond 8">Zond-8</a>\n</td>\n<td>5,375\n</td>\n<td>Proton\n</td>\n<td align="right">20 October 1970\n</td>\n<td>Circumlunar Loop\n</td>\n<td>non-human biological payload\n</td>\n<td><span style="color:green;">Success</span> &#8211; looped around Moon, returned biological payload safely to Earth despite landing off-target outside Soviet Union in the Indian Ocean\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Zond_5" title="Zond 5">Zond 5</a> was the first spacecraft to carry life from Earth to the vicinity of the Moon and return, initiating the final lap of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a> with its payload of tortoises, insects, plants, and bacteria. Despite the failure suffered in its final moments, the Zond 6 mission was reported by Soviet media as being a success as well. Although hailed worldwide as remarkable achievements, both of these Zond missions flew off-nominal reentry trajectories resulting in deceleration forces that would have been fatal to human crewmembers had they been aboard.\n</p><p>As a result, the Soviets secretly planned to continue unmanned Zond tests until their reliability to support manned flight had been demonstrated. However, due to NASA\'s continuing problems with the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Module" title="Apollo Lunar Module">lunar module</a>, and because of <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a> reports of a potential Soviet manned circumlunar flight in late 1968, NASA fatefully changed the flight plan of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a> from an Earth-orbit lunar module test to a lunar orbit mission scheduled for late December 1968.\n</p><p>In early December 1968 the launch window to the Moon opened for the Soviet launch site in <a href="/wiki/Baikonur" title="Baikonur">Baikonur</a>, giving the USSR their final chance to beat the US to the Moon. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmonauts" title="Cosmonauts">Cosmonauts</a> went on alert and asked to fly the Zond spacecraft then in final countdown at Baikonur on the first manned trip to the Moon. Ultimately, however, the Soviet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Politburo_of_the_Central_Committee_of_the_Communist_Party_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Politburo of the Central Committee of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union">Politburo</a> decided the risk of crew death was unacceptable given the combined poor performance to that point of Zond/Proton and so scrubbed the launch of a manned Soviet lunar mission. Their decision proved to be a wise one, since this unnumbered Zond mission was destroyed in another unmanned test when it was finally launched several weeks later.\n</p><p>By this time flights of the third generation U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Program" title="Apollo Program">Apollo</a> spacecraft had begun. Far more capable than the Zond, the Apollo spacecraft had the necessary rocket power to slip into and out of lunar orbit and to make course adjustments required for a safe reentry during the return to Earth. The <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">Apollo 8</a> mission carried out the first manned trip to the Moon on 24 December 1968, certifying the <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> booster for manned use and flying not a circumlunar loop but instead a full ten orbits around the Moon before returning safely to Earth. <a href="/wiki/Apollo_10" title="Apollo 10">Apollo 10</a> then performed a full dress rehearsal of a manned Moon landing in May 1969. This mission orbited within 47,400 feet (14.4&#160;km) altitude above the lunar surface, performing necessary low-altitude mapping of trajectory-altering mascons using a factory prototype lunar module that was too overweight to allow a successful landing. With the failure of the unmanned Soviet sample return Moon landing attempt <a href="/wiki/Luna_15" title="Luna 15">Luna 15</a> in July 1969, the stage was set for <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Manned_Moon_landings_.281969.E2.80.931972.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Manned_Moon_landings_(1969&#8211;1972)">Manned Moon landings (1969&#8211;1972)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Manned Moon landings (1969&#8211;1972)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_V_%26_N1_comparison.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="500" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Saturn_V_%26_N1_comparison.svg/250px-Saturn_V_%26_N1_comparison.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Saturn_V_%26_N1_comparison.svg/375px-Saturn_V_%26_N1_comparison.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Saturn_V_%26_N1_comparison.svg/500px-Saturn_V_%26_N1_comparison.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_V_%26_N1_comparison.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The U.S. <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> and the Soviet <a href="/wiki/N1_(rocket)" title="N1 (rocket)">N1</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="US_strategy">US strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: US strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program#Political_pressure_builds" title="Apollo program">Apollo program &#167;&#160;Political pressure builds</a></div>\n<p>Plans for manned Moon exploration originated during the <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Eisenhower</a> administration. In a series of mid-1950s articles in <i><a href="/wiki/Collier%27s" title="Collier\'s">Collier\'s</a></i> magazine, <a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a> had popularized the idea of a manned expedition to the Moon to establish a lunar base. A manned Moon landing posed several daunting technical challenges to the US and USSR. Besides guidance and weight management, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_re-entry" title="Atmospheric re-entry">atmospheric re-entry</a> without <a href="/wiki/Ablation" title="Ablation">ablative</a> overheating was a major hurdle. After the Soviet Union\'s launch of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sputnik" title="Sputnik">Sputnik</a>, von Braun promoted a plan for the United States Army to establish a military lunar outpost by 1965.\n</p><p>After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_race#Humans_in_space" title="Space race">early Soviet successes</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a>\'s flight, US President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> looked for a US project that would capture the public imagination. He asked Vice President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_Johnson" title="Lyndon Johnson">Lyndon Johnson</a> to make recommendations on a scientific endeavor that would prove US world leadership. The proposals included non-space options such as massive irrigation projects to benefit the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a>. The Soviets, at the time, had more powerful rockets than the United States, which gave them an advantage in some kinds of space mission.\n</p><p>Advances in US nuclear weapon technology had led to smaller, lighter warheads, and consequently, rockets with smaller payload capacities. By comparison, Soviet nuclear weapons were much heavier, and the powerful <a href="/wiki/R-7_Semyorka" title="R-7 Semyorka">R-7</a> rocket was developed to carry them. More modest potential missions such as flying around the Moon without landing or establishing a space lab in orbit (both were proposed by Kennedy to von Braun) were determined to offer too much advantage to the Soviets, since the US would have to develop a heavy rocket to match the Soviets. A Moon landing, however, would capture world imagination while functioning as propaganda.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_Landings_by_Nasa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="350" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Apollo_Landings_by_Nasa.jpg/350px-Apollo_Landings_by_Nasa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Apollo_Landings_by_Nasa.jpg/525px-Apollo_Landings_by_Nasa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Apollo_Landings_by_Nasa.jpg/700px-Apollo_Landings_by_Nasa.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_Landings_by_Nasa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Apollo landing sites</div></div></div>\n<p>Johnson had championed the US manned space program ever since the Sputnik scare, sponsoring the legislation which created NASA when he was in the Senate in 1958. When Kennedy asked him in 1961 to research the best manned space achievement to counter the Soviets\' lead, Johnson responded that the US had an even chance of beating the USSR to a manned lunar landing, but not for anything less. Kennedy seized on Apollo as the ideal focus for efforts in space. He ensured continuing funding, shielding space spending from the 1963 tax cut, but diverting money from other NASA scientific projects. This last dismayed NASA\'s leader, <a href="/wiki/James_E._Webb" title="James E. Webb">James E. Webb</a>, who perceived the need for NASA\'s support from the scientific community.\n</p><p>The Moon landing required development of the large Saturn V <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicle</a>, which achieved a perfect record of zero catastrophic failures or launch vehicle-caused mission failures, in thirteen launches.\n</p><p>For the program to succeed, its proponents would have to defeat criticism from politicians on the left, who wanted more money spent on social programs, and on those on the right, who favored a more military project. By emphasizing the scientific payoff and playing on fears of Soviet space dominance, Kennedy and Johnson managed to swing public opinion: by 1965, 58 percent of Americans favored Apollo, up from 33 percent two years earlier. After Johnson became President in 1963, his continuing defense of the program allowed it to succeed in 1969, as Kennedy planned.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_strategy">Soviet strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Soviet strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_manned_lunar_programs" title="Soviet manned lunar programs">Soviet Moonshot</a></div>\n<p>Soviet leader <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> said in October 1963 that the USSR was "not at present planning flight by cosmonauts to the Moon," while insisting that the Soviets had not dropped out of the race. Only after another year would the USSR fully commit itself to a Moon-landing attempt, which ultimately failed.\n</p><p>At the same time, Kennedy had suggested various joint programs, including a possible Moon landing by Soviet and U.S. astronauts and the development of better weather-monitoring satellites. Khrushchev, sensing an attempt by Kennedy to steal Russian space technology, rejected the idea: if the USSR went to the Moon, it would go alone. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sergey_Korolev" title="Sergey Korolev">Sergey Korolev</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_space_program" title="Soviet space program">Soviet space program</a>\'s chief designer, had started promoting his <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> craft and the <a href="/wiki/N1_(rocket)" title="N1 (rocket)">N1</a> launcher rocket that would have the capability of carrying out a manned Moon landing.\n</p><p>Khrushchev directed Korolev\'s design bureau to arrange further space firsts by modifying the existing Vostok technology, while a second team started building a completely new launcher and craft, the Proton booster and the Zond, for a manned cislunar flight in 1966. In 1964 the new Soviet leadership gave Korolev the backing for a Moon landing effort and brought all manned projects under his direction.\n</p><p>With Korolev\'s death and the failure of the first Soyuz flight in 1967, the co-ordination of the Soviet Moon landing program quickly unraveled. The Soviets built a landing craft and selected cosmonauts for the mission that would have placed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aleksei_Leonov" title="Aleksei Leonov">Aleksei Leonov</a> on the Moon\'s surface, but with the successive launch failures of the N1 booster in 1969, plans for a manned landing suffered first delay and then cancellation.\n</p><p>A program of automated return vehicles was begun, in the hope of being the first to return lunar rocks.  This had several failures.  It eventually succeeded with <a href="/wiki/Luna_16" title="Luna 16">Luna 16</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>  But this had little impact, because the Apollo 11 and Apollo 12 lunar landings and rock returns had already taken place by then.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apollo_missions">Apollo missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Apollo missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In total, twenty-four U.S. astronauts have traveled to the Moon. Three have made the trip twice, and twelve have walked on its surface. Apollo 8 was a lunar-orbit-only mission, Apollo 10 included undocking and Descent Orbit Insertion (DOI), followed by LM staging to CSM redocking, while Apollo 13, originally scheduled as a landing, ended up as a lunar fly-by, by means of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_return_trajectory" title="Free return trajectory">free return trajectory</a>; thus, none of these missions made landings. Apollo 7 and Apollo 9 were Earth-orbit-only missions. Apart from the inherent dangers of manned Moon expeditions as seen with Apollo 13, one reason for their cessation according to astronaut <a href="/wiki/Alan_Bean" title="Alan Bean">Alan Bean</a> is the cost it imposes in government subsidies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manned_Moon_landings">Manned Moon landings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Manned Moon landings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable" style="background: #f9f9f9; border-collapse: collapse;">\n<tbody><tr bgcolor="#efefef">\n<th>Mission name\n</th>\n<th>Lunar lander\n</th>\n<th width="11%">Lunar landing date\n</th>\n<th width="11%">Lunar liftoff date\n</th>\n<th width="12%">Lunar landing site\n</th>\n<th>Duration on lunar surface\n</th>\n<th width="25%">Crew\n</th>\n<th>Number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" title="Extra-vehicular activity">EVAs</a>\n</th>\n<th>Total EVA Time (HH:MM)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a>\n</td>\n<td><i>Eagle</i>\n</td>\n<td align="right">20 July 1969\n</td>\n<td align="right">21 July 1969\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Mare_Tranquillitatis" title="Mare Tranquillitatis">Sea of Tranquility</a>\n</td>\n<td>21:31\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Edwin "Buzz" Aldrin</a>\n</td>\n<td>1\n</td>\n<td>2:31\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Apollo_12" title="Apollo 12">Apollo 12</a>\n</td>\n<td><i>Intrepid</i>\n</td>\n<td align="right">19 November 1969\n</td>\n<td align="right">21 November 1969\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Oceanus_Procellarum" title="Oceanus Procellarum">Ocean of Storms</a>\n</td>\n<td>1-day, 7:31\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Pete_Conrad" title="Pete Conrad">Charles "Pete" Conrad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Bean" title="Alan Bean">Alan Bean</a>\n</td>\n<td>2\n</td>\n<td>7:45\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Apollo_14" title="Apollo 14">Apollo 14</a>\n</td>\n<td><i>Antares</i>\n</td>\n<td align="right">5 February 1971\n</td>\n<td align="right">6 February 1971\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro_formation" title="Fra Mauro formation">Fra Mauro</a>\n</td>\n<td>1-day, 9:30\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan B. align="right">24 April 1972\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Descartes_Highlands" title="Descartes Highlands">Descartes Highlands</a>\n</td>\n<td>2 days, 23:02\n</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugene_Cernan" title="Eugene Cernan">Eugene Cernan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harrison_Schmitt" title="Harrison Schmitt">Harrison "Jack" Schmitt</a>\n</td>\n<td>3\n</td>\n<td>22:04\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_aspects_of_the_Apollo_Moon_successful_landings">Other aspects of the Apollo Moon successful landings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other aspects of the Apollo Moon successful landings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Unlike other international rivalries, the Space Race has remained unaffected in a direct way regarding the desire for territorial expansion. After the successful landings on the Moon, the U.S. explicitly disclaimed the right to ownership of any part of the Moon.\n</p><p>President Richard Nixon had speechwriter <a href="/wiki/William_Safire" title="William Safire">William Safire</a> prepare a condolence speech for delivery in the event that Armstrong and Aldrin became marooned on the Moon\'s surface and could not be rescued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1951, science fiction writer <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">Arthur C. Clarke</a> forecast that man would reach the Moon by 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 16 August 2006, the <a href="/wiki/Associated_Press" title="Associated Press">Associated Press</a> reported that NASA is <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11_missing_tapes" title="Apollo 11 missing tapes">missing the original</a> <a href="/wiki/Slow-scan_television" title="Slow-scan television">Slow-scan television</a> tapes (which were made before the scan conversion for conventional TV) of the Apollo 11 Moon walk. Some news outlets have mistakenly reported that the SSTV tapes were found in Western Australia, but those tapes were only recordings of data from the Apollo 11 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Lunar_Surface_Experiment_Package" title="Apollo Lunar Surface Experiment Package">Early Apollo Surface Experiments Package</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scientists believe the six American flags planted by astronauts have been bleached white because of more than 40 years of exposure to solar radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-businessinsider_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-businessinsider-29">[29]</a></sup> Using <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter#LROC" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">LROC</a> images, five of the six American flags are still standing and casting shadows at all of the sites, except Apollo 11.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LROC_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-LROC-30">[30]</a></sup> Astronaut Buzz Aldrin reported that the flag was blown over by the exhaust from the ascent engine during liftoff of Apollo 11.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LROC_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-LROC-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Late_20th_century.E2.80.93Early_21st_century_unmanned_crash_landings"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_20th_century&#8211;Early_21st_century_unmanned_crash_landings">Late 20th century&#8211;Early 21st century unmanned crash landings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Late 20th century&#8211;Early 21st century unmanned crash landings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Hiten_.28Japan.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hiten_(Japan)">Hiten (Japan)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Hiten (Japan)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Launched on 24 January 1990, 11:46 UTC. At the end of its mission, the Japanese lunar orbiter <a href="/wiki/Hiten" title="Hiten">Hiten</a> was commanded to crash into the lunar surface and did so on 10 April 1993 at 18:03:25.7 UT (11 April 03:03:25.7 JST).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Lunar_Prospector_.28US.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunar_Prospector_(US)">Lunar Prospector (US)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Lunar Prospector (US)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Lunar_Prospector" title="Lunar Prospector">Lunar Prospector</a> was launched on 7 January 1998. The mission ended on 31 July 1999, when the orbiter was deliberately crashed into a crater near the lunar south pole after the presence of water ice was successfully detected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="SMART-1_.28ESA.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="SMART-1_(ESA)">SMART-1 (ESA)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: SMART-1 (ESA)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Launched 27 September 2003, 23:14 UTC from the Guiana Space Centre in Kourou, French Guiana. At the end of its mission, the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA</a> lunar orbiter <a href="/wiki/SMART-1" title="SMART-1">SMART-1</a> performed a controlled crash into the Moon, at about 2&#160;km/s. The time of the crash was 3 September 2006, at 5:42 UTC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="SELENE_.28Japan.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="SELENE_(Japan)">SELENE (Japan)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: SELENE (Japan)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/SELENE" title="SELENE">SELENE</a> or <i>Kaguya</i> was launched on 14 September 2007.  After successfully orbiting the Moon for a year and eight months, the main orbiter was instructed to impact on the lunar surface near the crater <a href="/wiki/Gill_(lunar_crater)" title="Gill (lunar crater)">Gill</a> at 18:25 UTC on 10 June 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lunarimpact_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-lunarimpact-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Chang.27e_1_.28China.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chang\'e_1_(China)">Chang\'e 1 (China)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Chang\'e 1 (China)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Chinese lunar orbiter <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_1" title="Chang\'e 1">Chang\'e 1</a>, launched 24 October 2007, 10:05 UTC, executed a controlled crash onto the surface of the Moon on 1 March 2009, 20:44 GMT, after a 16-month mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Chandrayaan-1_.28India.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chandrayaan-1_(India)">Chandrayaan-1 (India)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Chandrayaan-1 (India)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-1" title="Chandrayaan-1">Chandrayaan-1</a> was launched on 22 October 2008, 00:52 UTC. The impactor, the <a href="/wiki/Moon_Impact_Probe" title="Moon Impact Probe">Moon Impact Probe</a>, impacted near <a href="/wiki/Shackleton_(crater)" title="Shackleton (crater)">Shackleton</a> Crater at the south pole of the lunar surface at 14 November 2008, 20:31 IST.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times_Of_India_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Times_Of_India-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="LCROSS_.28US.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="LCROSS_(US)">LCROSS (US)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: LCROSS (US)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/LCROSS" title="LCROSS">LCROSS</a> data collecting shepherding spacecraft was launched together with the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter</a> (LRO) on 18 June 2009 on board an <a href="/wiki/Atlas_V" title="Atlas V">Atlas V</a> rocket with a <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(rocket_stage)" title="Centaur (rocket stage)">Centaur</a> upper stage. On 9 October 2009, at 11:31 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a>, the Centaur upper stage impacted the lunar surface, releasing the kinetic <a href="/wiki/TNT_equivalent" title="TNT equivalent">energy equivalent</a> of detonating approximately 2 tons of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinitrotoluene" title="Trinitrotoluene">TNT</a> (8.86 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigajoule" title="Gigajoule">GJ</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Six minutes later at 11:37 UTC, the LCROSS shepherding spacecraft also impacted the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="GRAIL_.28US.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="GRAIL_(US)">GRAIL (US)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: GRAIL (US)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity_Recovery_and_Interior_Laboratory" title="Gravity Recovery and Interior Laboratory">GRAIL</a> mission consisted of two small spacecraft: GRAIL A (<i>Ebb</i>), and GRAIL B (<i>Flow</i>). They were launched on 10 September 2011 on board a <a href="/wiki/Delta_II" title="Delta II">Delta II</a> rocket. GRAIL A separated from the rocket about nine minutes after launch, and GRAIL B followed about eight minutes later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The first probe entered orbit on 31 December 2011 and the second followed on 1 January 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The two spacecraft impacted the Lunar surface on 17 December 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="LADEE_.28US.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="LADEE_(US)">LADEE (US)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: LADEE (US)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer" title="Lunar Atmosphere and Dust Environment Explorer">LADEE</a> was launched on 7 September 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gsfc-ladee_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-gsfc-ladee-43">[43]</a></sup> The mission ended on 18 April 2014, when the spacecraft\'s controllers intentionally crashed LADEE into the <a href="/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon" title="Far side of the Moon">far side of the Moon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt20140418_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20140418-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc20140418_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc20140418-45">[45]</a></sup> which, later, was determined to be near the eastern rim of <a href="/wiki/Sundman_(crater)" title="Sundman (crater)">Sundman V crater</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20141028-NNJ_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20141028-NNJ-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20140418_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20140418-47">[47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century_unmanned_soft_landings">21st century unmanned soft landings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: 21st century unmanned soft landings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Chang.27e_3_.28China.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chang\'e_3_(China)">Chang\'e 3 (China)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Chang\'e 3 (China)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 14 December 2013 at 13:12 UTC<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_3" title="Chang\'e 3">Chang\'e 3</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_landing_(rocketry)" title="Soft landing (rocketry)">soft-landed</a> a <a href="/wiki/Yutu_(rover)" title="Yutu (rover)">rover</a> on the Moon. This was the first lunar soft landing since <a href="/wiki/Luna_24" title="Luna 24">Luna 24</a> on 22 August 1976.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Chang.27e_4_.28China.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chang\'e_4_(China)">Chang\'e 4 (China)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Chang\'e 4 (China)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 3 January 2019 at 2:26&#160;UTC Chang\'e 4 became the first spacecraft to land on the far side of the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Landings_on_moons_of_other_Solar_System_bodies">Landings on moons of other Solar System bodies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Landings on moons of other Solar System bodies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Progress in <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">space exploration</a> has recently broadened the phrase <i>moon landing</i> to include other moons in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> as well. The <a href="/wiki/Huygens_(spacecraft)" title="Huygens (spacecraft)"><i>Huygens</i> probe</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini&#8211;Huygens</a></i> mission to <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> performed a successful unmanned moon landing on <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> in 2005. Similarly, the Soviet probe <a href="/wiki/Phobos_2" title="Phobos 2">Phobos 2</a> came within 120&#160;mi (190&#160;km) of performing an unmanned moon landing on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>\' moon <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a> in 1989 before radio contact with that lander was suddenly lost. A similar Russian sample return mission called <a href="/wiki/Fobos-Grunt" title="Fobos-Grunt">Fobos-Grunt</a> ("grunt" means "soil" in Russian) launched in November 2011, but stalled in low Earth orbit. There is widespread interest in performing a future moon landing on <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a> to drill down and explore the possible liquid water ocean beneath its icy surface.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_future_missions">Proposed future missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Proposed future missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3967" data-file-width="3929" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg/220px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg/330px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg/440px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Astronaut <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Buzz Aldrin</a>, Lunar Module pilot of the first lunar landing mission, poses for a photograph beside the deployed <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_the_United_States" title="Flag of the United States">United States flag</a> during an Apollo 11 Extravehicular Activity (EVA) on the lunar surface.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_proposed_missions_to_the_Moon" title="List of proposed missions to the Moon">List of proposed missions to the Moon</a></div>\n<p>China is planning to land and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sample_return_mission" title="Sample return mission">return lunar soil samples</a> by 2020 in the <a href="/wiki/Chang%27e_5" title="Chang\'e 5">Chang\'e 5</a> mission.\n</p><p>Israel SpaceIL is launching on Feb. 22, 2019 the "Beresheet" lunar lander. The craft will be launched from Cape Canaveral, Florida, by SpaceX Falcon 9; one of several payloads aboard the SpaceX rocket. "Beresheet" will get to the moon using a slow but energy efficient trajectory. That is, it will first achieve a large elliptical orbit around earth with a diameter around 200,000 KM. At that point it can be caught by the moon\'s gravity and enter the moon\'s orbit, where it will slow down over several weeks before the eventual landing in late April. If successful, the mission will be the <a class="external text" href="https://www.nbcnews.com/mach/science/israeli-start-aims-put-first-private-space-probe-moon-ncna971556" rel="nofollow">first privately funded lunar landing</a>. <a href="/wiki/SpaceIL" title="SpaceIL">SpaceIL</a> was originally conceived in 2011 as a venture to pursue Google\'s Lunar X Prize.\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISRO" title="ISRO">ISRO</a>, the Indian National Space agency, is planning a second version of Chandrayaan named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chandrayaan_2" title="Chandrayaan 2">Chandrayaan 2</a>. According to former ISRO Chairman <a href="/wiki/G._Madhavan_Nair" title="G. Madhavan Nair">G. Madhavan Nair</a>, "The <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">Indian Space Research Organisation</a> (ISRO) hopes to land two rovers &#8211; one Indian and another Russian &#8211; on the Moon in 2019, as a part of its second <a href="/wiki/Chandrayaan-2" title="Chandrayaan-2">Chandrayaan</a> mission. The rover will be designed to move on wheels on the lunar surface, pick up samples of soil or rocks, do on-site chemical analysis and send the data to the mother-spacecraft Chandrayaan II, which will be orbiting above. Chandrayaan II will transmit the data to Earth."  The payloads have already been finalized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-payload_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-payload-52">[52]</a></sup> ISRO has mentioned that due to weight restrictions it will not be carrying any overseas payloads on this mission. The lander, named Vikram, is projected to weigh 1,250&#160;kg, and the entire spacecraft will be launched by the <a href="/wiki/Geosynchronous_Satellite_Launch_Vehicle" title="Geosynchronous Satellite Launch Vehicle">Geosynchronous Satellite Launch Vehicle</a>.\n</p><p>Russia\'s <a href="/wiki/Luna-Glob" title="Luna-Glob">Luna-Glob 1</a> was expected to be launched in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-launch2018_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-launch2018-53">[53]</a></sup> In 2007 the head of the Russian Space Agency announced plans to send cosmonauts to the Moon by 2025 and establish a permanent robotically operated base there in 2027&#8211;2032.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In 2015, Roscosmos stated that Russia plans to place a cosmonaut on the Moon by 2030, leaving Mars to <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>. The purpose is to work jointly with NASA and avoid another <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Precursor_Robotic_Program" title="Lunar Precursor Robotic Program">Lunar Precursor Robotic Program</a> (LPRP) was a program of <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">robotic spacecraft</a> missions which NASA will use to prepare for future Moon landings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Three orbiters have been launched in the program, the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter</a> (LRO), the <a href="/wiki/LCROSS" title="LCROSS">Lunar Crater Observation and Sensing Satellite</a> (LCROSS), and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Atmosphere_and_Dust_Environment_Explorer" title="Lunar Atmosphere and Dust Environment Explorer">Lunar Atmosphere and Dust Environment Explorer</a> (LADEE), launched in 2013, but no Moon landings are scheduled yet.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Google_Lunar_X_Prize" title="Google Lunar X Prize">Google Lunar X Prize</a> competition offered a $20&#160;million award for the first privately funded team to land a robotic probe on the Moon. Like the <a href="/wiki/Ansari_X_Prize" title="Ansari X Prize">Ansari X Prize</a> before it, the competition aimed to advance the state of the art in private space exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Of the several competing teams, <a href="/wiki/Puli_Space_Technologies" title="Puli Space Technologies">Puli Space Technologies</a> at one time (2012) planned to launch in 2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated. (March 2015)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>\nand <a href="/wiki/Astrobotic_Technology" title="Astrobotic Technology">Astrobotic Technology</a> planned to launch in the second half of 2016 with their own rover plus another team\'s lunar rovers from <a href="/wiki/Hakuto" title="Hakuto">Hakuto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glxp20150223_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-glxp20150223-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A consortium of Vodafone, Audi and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=PT_Scientists&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="PT Scientists (page does not exist)">PT Scientists</a> is planning a Moon landing in 2019. The mission is to launch a lander and two small rovers to the Moon from Cape Canaveral, Florida, on a SpaceX Falcon 9 rocket during the 50th-anniversary year of NASA\'s Apollo 11 Moon landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Nokia will assist to set up an LTE network to manage lunar terrestrial communication system of the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_empirical_evidence">Historical empirical evidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Historical empirical evidence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing_conspiracy_theories" title="Moon landing conspiracy theories">Moon landing conspiracy theories</a></div>\n<p>Many conspiracy theorists hold that the Apollo Moon landings were a hoax;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> however, <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empirical</a> evidence is readily available to show that <a href="/wiki/Third-party_evidence_for_Apollo_Moon_landings" title="Third-party evidence for Apollo Moon landings">manned Moon landings did occur</a>. Anyone on Earth with an appropriate <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a> system can bounce laser beams off three <a href="/wiki/Retroreflector" title="Retroreflector">retroreflector</a> arrays left on the Moon by Apollo 11,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> 14 and 15, verifying deployment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment" title="Lunar Laser Ranging Experiment">Lunar Laser Ranging Experiment</a> at historically documented Apollo Moon landing sites and so proving equipment constructed on Earth was successfully transported to the surface of the Moon. In addition, in August 2009 NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter" title="Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter">Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter</a> began to send back high resolution photos of the Apollo landing sites. These photos show not only the large Descent Stages of the lunar landers left behind but also tracks of the astronauts\' walking paths in the lunar dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moon_landing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" Moon (disambiguation)</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References_and_notes">References and notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
